                .addSize([0, 0], [640, 480])
                .build()

        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

        googletag.pubads()
            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/adfm.202303102')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-leaderboard')
            .defineSizeMapping(sizes.leaderboard)
            .addService(googletag.pubads())
            .setCollapseEmptyDiv(true);

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="f1dbe12dd214487db98f6cb0c2d6efb3">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '062b9ec8-ec29-4804-8c2b-781054218f79'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

empty"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-chain"></i><h2>References</h2></a></li><li role="presentation" class="tab__nav__item"><a id="pane-pcw-relatedcon-cloned" href="#pane-pcw-related" data-slide-clone="self" aria-controls="pane-pcw-related" role="menuitem" data-slide-target="#pane-pcw-related" class="tab__nav__item__link article-row-right__tabLinks w-slide__btn related-tab cloned-with-ids"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-related-circles-extend-button"></i><h2>Related</h2></a></li><li role="presentation" class="tab__nav__item"><a id="pane-pcw-detailscon-cloned" href="#pane-pcw-details" data-slide-clone="self" aria-controls="pane-pcw-details" role="menuitem" data-slide-target="#pane-pcw-details" class="tab__nav__item__link article-row-right__tabLinks    <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d217230491" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Pre-intercalating ions between V<span class="icomoon"></span>O layers is considered to be an effective strategy to modulate the interlayer spacing of 2D vanadium oxides. However, the rigid pre-intercalated ions hardly keep stable during repeated charging/discharging process and their sizes limit the extent of interlayer spacing expansion, which inevitably lead to poor rate capability and cycle stability. In this work, aliphatic diamines are adopted as pre-intercalated guests to elastically modulate the interlayer spacing of V<span class="icomoon"></span>O layers by tuning the chain length of the organic diamine molecules. Benefiting from the strong interaction between the terminal doubly protonated amine and the polar negative oxygen bridge of the V<span class="icomoon"></span>O layers, the aliphatic diamine molecules can act as a structural stabilizer between the layers and boost fast Na ion diffusion (10<sup>−8</sup> to 10<sup>−10</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>). The sodium ion battery based on the first synthesized 1,6-hexanediamine pre-intercalated vanadium oxide supported on nickel foam hybrid cathode achieves a large specific capacity of 597 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.09 A g<sup>−1</sup>, as well as superior rate performance and cycling stability. This work provides a strategy to elastically modulate 2D layered materials with tunable interlayer spacing for batteries based on large-size-ions.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202303102-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202303102-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Lithium ion batteries (LIBs) have dominated the worldwide markets of portable/wearable electronic devices and electric vehicles for decades in virtue of their high energy density and long lifespan.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0001_R_d217230257e456" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nature">1</a>, <a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0002_R_d217230257e456" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the high cost of lithium hinders the sustainable application of LIBs in large-scale energy storage. To this end, exploiting sustainable energy storage technologies with low cost and high capacity to replace LIBs has become a hot research topic in the field of energy storage. Benefiting from the analogous chemical properties and energy storage mechanism as lithium as well as the low cost (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303102-fig-0001">1a</a>; Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0003_R_d217230257e471" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Mater.">3</a>-<a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0006_R_d217230257e471" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Energy Environ. Sci.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> sodium ion batteries (SIBs) are considered to be qualified to fulfill the large-scale energy storage requirements and to be a promising alternative to LIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0007_R_d217230257e476" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Front Chem">7</a>-<a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0011_R_d217230257e476" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Energy Storage Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Even compared with many other emerging green batteries based on K<sup>+</sup> ,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0012_R_d217230257e484" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nat. Commun.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Mg<sup>2+</sup> ,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0013_R_d217230257e491" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Energy Storage Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Zn<sup>2+</sup>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0014_R_d217230257e499" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">14</a>, <a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0015_R_d217230257e499" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Mater.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and Al<sup>3+[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0016_R_d217230257e504" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nat. Commun.">16</a>, <a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0017_R_d217230257e504" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> etc., SIBs still have great appeal in terms of cost and maturity of fabrication technology.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202303102-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5ce89c99-edbc-4f36-8c29-81442149d616/adfm202303102-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/5ce89c99-edbc-4f36-8c29-81442149d616/adfm202303102-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4d2bbf85-016f-4998-8529-1d09c13ecb03/adfm202303102-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5ce89c99-edbc-4f36-8c29-81442149d616/adfm202303102-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202303102-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202303102" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Comparisons of the cost, abundance and intrinsic attribute of Na and Li. b) Various metal ions as pre-intercalated guests between V<span class="icomoon"></span>O layers. Layer structure of c) PDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO, d) BDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO and e) HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO specimen. Polyhedral representations of vanadium oxide layers of f) PDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO, g) BDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO and h) HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO specimen.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The only fly in the ointment is the larger radius of sodium ions than that of lithium ions (Na 1.02&nbsp;Å versus Li 0.76&nbsp;Å),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0018_R_d217230257e531" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Mater.">18</a>, <a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0019_R_d217230257e531" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Nano Energy">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which will slow down the diffusion of sodium ions inside the active materials and distort the material structure,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0020_R_d217230257e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">20</a>, <a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0021_R_d217230257e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Nat. Commun.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> hindering the simultaneous realization of high capacity, high energy/power density, and superb rate/cycling performance. Therefore, to achieve excellent sodium storage performances, materials with large spacing and open structures are expected to be the best option as carrier hosts, such as transition metal oxides (TMOs),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0022_R_d217230257e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nat. Mater.">22</a>-<a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0025_R_d217230257e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nat. Mater.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Prussian blue analogues<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0026_R_d217230257e547" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">26</a>-<a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0028_R_d217230257e547" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and fluorophosphates.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0029_R_d217230257e552" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">29</a>-<a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0031_R_d217230257e552" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among various TMOs, vanadium-based oxides are considered to be promising candidates for ion storage due to the multi-electron redox activity of vanadium (V<sup>5+</sup> to V<sup>2+</sup>) and their 2D layered structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0014_R_d217230257e562" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">14</a>, <a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0032_R_d217230257e562" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Sci.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, their narrow interlayer spacing and structural unstability, i.e., irreversible phase transitions and dissolution, during the repeated insertion/extraction of large-sized Na<sup>+</sup> ions severely limit the achievement of their superior electrochemical performances. Encouragingly, <sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0033_R_d217230257e569" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the interlayer spacing of 2D vanadium-based oxides can be facilely tuned by pre-inserting ions or molecules between V<span class="icomoon"></span>O layers. Currently, the most prevalent used pre-intercalated guests are metal ions with various sizes, e.g. K<sup>+</sup>, Na<sup>+</sup>, Li<sup>+</sup>, Al<sup>3+</sup>, etc. However, in many cases, due to the rigid nature and weak interaction with the lattice framework hosts, the pre-intercalated ion guest columns would irreversibly detach during charging, leading to the collapse of host structure and deterioration of the cycle stability. At the same time, the extent of interlayer spacing expansion is also limited by the intrinsic size of pre-intercalated ions (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303102-fig-0001">1b</a>). In order to realize the elastic regulation of interlayer spacing in a wide range, it is of great practical significance to explore the dimensionally controllable and structurally stable pre-intercalated guests. Organic molecules, by virtue of their higher operating voltage and better phase transition inhibition, can be used as pre-intercalated guest materials to modulate the interlayer spacing and stabilize the host structures, providing sufficient sodium storage space and fast transport channels.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0034_R_d217230257e587" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Mater.">34</a>-<a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0037_R_d217230257e587" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Mater.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among them, aliphatic diamine can form strong hydrogen bonds with the V<span class="icomoon"></span>O layers when pre-inserted between the 2D layered vanadium-based oxide layers due to their two –NH<sub>2</sub> end groups, while there is an electrostatic interaction between the terminal doubly protonated amino group and the negative V<span class="icomoon"></span>O layers. Thus, these two organic-inorganic interfacial interactions enable the aliphatic diamine to work as a stable pillar to extend the interlayer spacing of V<span class="icomoon"></span>O layers, and the resultant hybrid electrodes have a dual energy storage mechanism that can achieve both high specific capacity and long lifespan.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0034_R_d217230257e594" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Mater.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In terms of electrode structure, conventional coating method will inevitably cause a large internal resistance due to the introduction of insulative polymer binders, which will also encapsulate the active materials to a certain extent and ultimately limit the actual performance. To solve this problem, vanadium-based oxides can be in situ grown on a 3D porous conductive current collector, thus simultaneously improving the electron and ion transport in the electrode system, achieving efficient and stable energy storage.</p>
         
         <p>Here, a judicious strategy to facilely tune the interlayer spacing of layered vanadium-based oxides through a simple solvothermal method is proposed (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). Using aliphatic diamines with different chain lengths (1,3-propanediamine, 1,4-butanediamine, 1,6-hexanediamine) as pre-intercalated guests, three organic-inorganic hybrid materials, i.e., (H<sub>3</sub>N(CH<sub>2</sub>)<sub>3</sub>NH<sub>3</sub>)[V<sub>4</sub>O<sub>10</sub>] (denoted as PDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO), (H<sub>3</sub>N(CH<sub>2</sub>)<sub>4</sub>NH<sub>3</sub>)[V<sub>6</sub>O<sub>14</sub>] (denoted as BDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO) and (H<sub>3</sub>N(CH<sub>2</sub>)<sub>6</sub>NH<sub>3</sub>)[V<sub>6</sub>O<sub>14</sub>] (denoted as HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO) are in situ synthesized on nickel foam current collectors. With the increase of the template chain length, the V<span class="icomoon"></span>O layer evolves from a planar [V<sub>4</sub>O<sub>10</sub>] to a serrated [V<sub>6</sub>O<sub>14</sub>] and the interlayer spacing is expanded sequentially. Benefiting from the serrated structure and enlarged interlayer spacing that favor the massive storage and fast transport of sodium ions, the first synthesized HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO hybrid cathode delivers a specific capacity of up to 597&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at a current density of 0.09&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, which can well fulfill the practical requirements of large-scale high-density energy storage. The capacity retains 233&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> when the current density increases 32 times to 2.88&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. The hybrid cathode also exhibits superior cycling stability, with only 0.051% capacity decay per cycle at 10&nbsp;C. Our work develops a functionally oriented methodology for the synthesis of organic-inorganic hybrid layered materials with tunable interlayer structure that provides high-density and ultrastable energy storage.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202303102-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202303102-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202303102-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202303102-sec-0030-title">2.1 Crystal Structure of DA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO</h3>
            
            <p>The crystal structures of PDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO, BDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO, and HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO specimens are displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303102-fig-0001">1c–h</a>, all of which are composed of common inorganic building blocks, i.e., square pyramidal {V<sup>IV</sup>O<sub>5</sub>}<sub>sp</sub> and tetrahedral {V<sup>V</sup>O<sub>4</sub>}<sub>tet</sub>. The {V<sup>IV</sup>O<sub>5</sub>}<sub>sp</sub> possesses four long V<span class="icomoon"></span>O bonds and one short V = O bond while {V<sup>V</sup>O<sub>4</sub>}<sub>tet</sub> has three long V<span class="icomoon"></span>O bonds and one short V<span class="icomoon"></span>O bond. Due to the structural orientation effect of the aliphatic diamines, different layered structures can be formed. Aliphatic diamines with longer carbon chain are apt to regulate the {V<sup>IV</sup>O<sub>5</sub>}<sub>sp</sub> and {V<sup>V</sup>O<sub>4</sub>}<sub>tet</sub> building blocks to assemble serrated structure while the ones with shorter carbon chain tend to form planar structure. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303102-fig-0001">1c,f</a>, each [V<sub>2</sub><sup>IV</sup>O<sub>8</sub>]<sup>2−</sup> dimer is surrounded by six {V<sup>V</sup>O<sub>4</sub>}<sub>tet</sub> units, and three oxygen atoms in each {V<sup>V</sup>O<sub>4</sub>}<sub>tet</sub> unit are shared with the adjacent dimer, thus forming the PDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO with planar structure. In sharp contrast, when the adjacent [V<sub>2</sub><sup>IV</sup>O<sub>8</sub>]<sup>2−</sup> dimers share their edges in a maleinoid form, 1D {[V<sub>2</sub><sup>IV</sup>O<sub>8</sub>]<sup>2−</sup>}<i><sub>n</sub></i> zigzag chains can be formed and connected by {V<sup>V</sup>O<sub>4</sub>}<sub>tet</sub> units to construct a [V<sub>4</sub><sup>IV</sup>O<sub>10</sub>V<sub>2</sub><sup>V</sup>O<sub>4</sub>]<sup>2−</sup> 2D twisting lamellar structure, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303102-fig-0001">1d,g</a> for BDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO and Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303102-fig-0001">1e,h</a> for HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0038_R_d217230257e785" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - J. Inorg. Organomet. Polym.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>The crystal structure of HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO insitu grown on the nickel foam (NF) is confirmed by powder X-ray diffraction (PXRD), as shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303102-fig-0002">2a</a>. The sharp characteristic peak at 2&nbsp;=&nbsp;9.9°, 14.2°, 19.9°, 21.6°, 27.5°, and 28.9° corresponds to the (−101), (002), (−202), (−103), (−11–3), and (1–21) crystal plane of HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO, respectively. The pattern is well in line with the single-crystal simulation, and the absence of any other peaks indicates the high purity of the resultant. In this work, HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO was synthesized for the first time and the detailed crystal information of HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO is summarized in Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2–S4</a> (Supporting Information). Compared with HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO, the XRD pattern of PDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO and BDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO displays similar characteristic peaks but with larger diffraction angles, indicating that interlayer spacing of V<span class="icomoon"></span>O layers could be precisely and facilely tuned by changing the chain length of the pre-intercalated organic diamine. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303102-fig-0002">2b</a>, when 1,3-propanediamine, 1,4-butanediamine and 1,6-hexanediamine guest pre-inserts into the 2D layered vanadium-based oxides, the interlayer spacing gradually increases from 7.7&nbsp;Å (2<i>θ</i>&nbsp;=&nbsp;11.5°), through 8.1&nbsp;Å (2<i>θ</i>&nbsp;=&nbsp;10.9°), and finally to 8.9&nbsp;Å (2<i>θ</i>&nbsp;=&nbsp;9.9°), which perfectly obeys the linear relationship with the number of carbon atoms in the aliphatic diamines (inset of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303102-fig-0002">2b</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0034_R_d217230257e814" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Mater.">34</a>, <a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0039_R_d217230257e814" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Energy Environ. Sci">39</a>, <a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0035_R_d217230257e814" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Chem">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To demonstrate the inertness of NF skeleton during the synthesis process, PDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO, BDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO, and HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO powders are peeled off from NF skeleton by ultrasonic and conducted the XRD characterization. The patterns of the powder specimens are well in accordance with the hybrid ones (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). Fourier transform infrared spectra (FTIR) of PDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO, BDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO and HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO specimens are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information). The characteristic peaks in the range of 500–1000&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> are attributed to various vibrations of V<span class="icomoon"></span>O bonds while the ones near 986&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> belong to V≐O stretching vibrations.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0040_R_d217230257e830" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Nano Lett.">40</a>-<a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0042_R_d217230257e830" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - J. Power Sources">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The bands in the range of 1390–1467&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> are assigned to characteristic peaks of C<span class="icomoon"></span>N, and the bands in the range 1650–1590&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> are attributed to the bending vibrations of N<span class="icomoon"></span>H. The protonated [<span class="icomoon"></span>NH<sub>3</sub>]<sup>+</sup> in the range 2904–3074&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> indicates the successful pre-insertion of aliphatic diamines guests in V<span class="icomoon"></span>O layers of PDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO, BDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO, and HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO hosts. The similar patterns of these specimens demonstrate that the change of the organic diamines chain does not have a large effect on the vibrational peaks. Raman spectra are further conducted, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information). The peak positions of these specimens are generally consistent. Two low-frequency peaks at 189 and 136&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> attribute to the stretching mode of (V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>)<i><sub>n</sub></i> and are strongly associated with the layered structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0043_R_d217230257e859" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Spectrochim. Acta, Part A">43</a>, <a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0044_R_d217230257e859" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Solid State Ionics">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Other characteristic peaks at 407&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> and 279&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, 522&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, 689&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> and 991&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> are attributed to the bending vibrations of V≐O, tri-ligand oxygen (V<sub>3</sub><span class="icomoon"></span>O) stretching mode, double-ligand oxygen (V<sub>2</sub><span class="icomoon"></span>O) stretching mode and terminal oxygen (V≐O) stretching mode. Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) with a heating rate of 10°C&nbsp;min<sup>−1</sup> in air records an obvious weight loss between 300–400&nbsp;°C, originates from the removal of organic molecules. The total weight loss of 18%, 14%, and 19% corresponds to the weight percentage of pre-intercalated aliphatic diamines in the PDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO, BDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO, and HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO hosts, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). Comparison of survey spectrum of PDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO, BDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO, and HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO with bare V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> was conducted, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303102-fig-0002">2c</a>, the emerging strong N 1s signal and progressive enhanced C 1s signal of PDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO, BDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO, and HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO convincingly verifies the successful embeddedness of aliphatic diamines in V<span class="icomoon"></span>O layers. The microstructure of HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF hybrid electrode is investigated by scanning electron microscopy (SEM) characterization. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6e</a> (Supporting Information), the pristine open structure of the NF is well preserved after HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO combination. HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO crystals exhibit nanosheets morphology with a thickness of ≈20&nbsp;nm, vertically and evenly aligned on the 3D NF skeleton, which facilitates charge transfer from the active material to the current collector and allows fast ion transport to the electrode/electrolyte interface without introducing any insulative binders used in conventional coating method, reducing the contact resistances between the components for efficient and stable energy storage (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303102-fig-0002">2d,e</a>). The PDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO and BDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO electrodes also exhibit a similar nanosheets morphology with the thickness of ≈30&nbsp;nm and ≈20&nbsp;nm, respectively, inferring that the regulation of the organic diamines chain has negligible influence on the morphology (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6a–d</a>, Supporting Information). In addition, transmission electron microscopy (TEM) characterization verifies the 2D layered structure of HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO specimen (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303102-fig-0002">2f</a>). The large interlayer spacing favors the massive storage and fast transport of sodium ions, helping to simultaneously achieve high capacity and superior rate/cycling performance. The element mapping reveals the presence of C, N, O, and V elements and their uniform distribution on the NF skeleton, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303102-fig-0002">2g</a>.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202303102-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/cf13882a-0b32-4f87-b78b-2cecb9bfea8f/adfm202303102-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/cf13882a-0b32-4f87-b78b-2cecb9bfea8f/adfm202303102-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/dd3772db-d6c9-4f26-ac2e-7b0a9d6a158d/adfm202303102-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/cf13882a-0b32-4f87-b78b-2cecb9bfea8f/adfm202303102-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202303102-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202303102-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Performance</h3>
            
            <p>To evaluate the sodium storage performance of PDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF, BDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF, and HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF hybrid electrodes, a series of coin-type SIBs are assembled. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303102-fig-0003">3a</a> shows the first four cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of the HDA−VO/NF electrode at a scan rate of 0.2&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. It can be clearly seen that the CV curve of the first cycle is significantly different from the subsequent ones, which is attributed to the irreversible formation of solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) film on the electrode surface during sodium insertion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0045_R_d217230257e949" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - J. Power Sources">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> From the second cycle, the curves display a pair of reversible redox peaks of 1.7/0.83&nbsp;V. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303102-fig-0003">3b</a> compares the CV curves of these three specimens at 0.2&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. Relatives to the PDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF and BDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF counterparts, the HDA−VO/NF with largest interlayer spacing (8.9&nbsp;Å) has the largest peak current density and integral area, showing the potential to achieve the highest capacity. Moreover, compared with the reversible redox peaks of PDA−VO/NF (1.76/0.85&nbsp;V) and BDA−VO/NF (1.65/0.52&nbsp;V), the smallest interval between the redox peaks in HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF indicates its least polarization and minimum resistances for better charge storage performance. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303102-fig-0003">3c</a> shows the galvanostatic charge/discharge (GCD) curves of the HDA−VO/NF electrode for the first four cycles at current density of 0.09&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. The HDA−VO/NF electrode delivers a specific capacity of 865 and 476&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> in the first discharge and charge, with a Coulombic efficiency (CE) of 55%. The initial low CE is mainly attributed to the formation of SEI film on the electrode surface. In the subsequent cycles, the discharge and charge curves overlap well, and the CE reaches as high as ≈100%, indicating the high reversibility of Na<sup>+</sup> storage in layered HDA−VO. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303102-fig-0003">3d</a> compares the GCD curves of PDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF (7.7&nbsp;Å), BDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF (8.1&nbsp;Å), and HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF (8.9&nbsp;Å) electrodes at their initial current densities. The discharge specific capacity is 250, 399, and 601&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, which is well consistent with the increasing trend of their interlayer spacing, proving once again that large interlayer spacing can promote the massive Na<sup>+</sup> storage. In addition, HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF electrode possesses the smallest interval between charging and discharging plateaus, which is in good agreement with the redox peaks of the CV curves.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202303102-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d90bfe3d-0bdf-4a93-afc4-7cf073f286c0/adfm202303102-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/d90bfe3d-0bdf-4a93-afc4-7cf073f286c0/adfm202303102-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/cd17cd02-1e03-4763-81c6-1ad5c37a6d07/adfm202303102-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d90bfe3d-0bdf-4a93-afc4-7cf073f286c0/adfm202303102-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202303102-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202303102" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV curves of HDA−VO/Na half cell in the first four cycles at 0.2&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> within 0.01−3.0&nbsp;V. b) CV curves comparison of half cells based on PDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF, BDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF, and HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF at 0.2&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. c) GCD curves of HDA−VO/Na half cell in the first four cycles at a current density of 0.09&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. d) GCD curves comparison of half cells based on PDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF, BDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF, and HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF at the initial current density. e) Rate performance of PDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF, BDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF, and HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF hybrid electrodes. f) Cycling performance of PDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF, BDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF, and HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF with bare V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> hybrid electrodes at 1&nbsp;C for 300 cycles. g) Comparison of the specific capacity of the PDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF, BDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF, and HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF hybrid electrodes in this work and recently reported vanadium-based electrode materials applied in SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0046" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0046_R_d217230257e1008" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Adv. Energy Mater.">46</a>-<a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0058" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0058_R_d217230257e1008" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Adv. Energy Mater.">58</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Rate capability of PDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF, BDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF, and HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF hybrid electrodes is compared in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303102-fig-0003">3e</a>. Benefiting from the serrated V<span class="icomoon"></span>O layer structure having more active sites and the largest interlayer spacing facilitating Na<sup>+</sup> ion storage and transport, the SIB based on HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF electrode delivers the highest specific capacity of 597&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at a current density of 0.09&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, overwhelming those of the PDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF (249&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) and BDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF (370&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) counterparts at smaller 0.07&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. At the subsequent current densities of 0.18, 0.36, 0.72, 1.44, and 2.88&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF electrode still maintains its specific capacities of 482, 435, 359, 304, and 233&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, which are much higher than those of PDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF and BDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF and well fit our assumptions. When the current density returns to the initial 0.09&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF electrode can recover to 85% of its original capacity, proving its superior structural stability originates from the pre-intercalated aliphatic diamines guest. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303102-fig-0003">3g</a> shows a comparative graph of the specific capacity of PDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF, BDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF, and HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF hybrid electrodes in this work and recently reported vanadium-based electrode materials applied in SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0046_R_d217230257e1041" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Adv. Energy Mater.">46</a>-<a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0058_R_d217230257e1041" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Adv. Energy Mater.">58</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thanks to the serrated V<span class="icomoon"></span>O layer structure and the tuned interlayer spacing of HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO, as well as the introduction of 3D high-conductive NF skeleton that improves the electron/ion transport, the HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF hybrid electrode delivers the highest specific capacity relatives to other counterparts (Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). Cycling stability test of PDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF, BDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF and HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF with bare V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> hybrid electrodes is performed at a current density of 1&nbsp;C, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303102-fig-0003">3f</a>. The HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF electrode delivers an initial discharge specific capacity of 449&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (0.36&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for 1&nbsp;C) and retains ≈269&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 300 cycles, with a retention rate of 60%, which is close to that of BDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF (67%) and much better than those of PDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF (42%) and bare V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> (23%). In addition, the CE of HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF maintains ≈100% throughout the whole cycling process. The superiority of cycling stability in HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF is more obvious when applying a much higher current density, such as 10&nbsp;C. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information), the HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF electrode exhibits an initial discharge specific capacity of 293&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (3.6&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for 10&nbsp;<i>C</i>) and retains ≈145&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles, with a retention rate of 49%, which outperforms those of BDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF (27%), PDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF (27%) and bare V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> (25%). The morphological evolution of PDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO, BDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO and HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO at the fully discharged and charged state and after long 300-cycles are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information). The morphology of HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO remains unchanged, displaying the pristine nanosheet structure, while the ones of PDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO and BDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO present the phenomenon of agglomeration, cracking and pulverization, which are caused by the instability of the structure due to insufficient interlayer spacing during Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation/extraction. These results demonstrate that the cycling stability can be remarkably enhanced by expanding the interlayer spacing of V<span class="icomoon"></span>O layers through increasing the length of carbon chain in aliphatic diamines guests, thus exploiting sufficient rooms for sodium ion storage and diffusion.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202303102-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202303102-sec-0050-title">2.3 Reaction/Diffusion Kinetic Mechanism</h3>
            
            <p>To reveal the kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusing inside the hybrid electrode and charge transfer at the electrode/electrolyte interface, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) of PDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO, BDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO, and HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO hybrid electrodes before and after cycling is investigated in the frequency range from 100&nbsp;kHz to 10&nbsp;mHz and the fitted equivalent circuit is shown in the inset of <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303102-fig-0004">4b</a>. It can be seen from the Nyquist plot that the impedance is composed of an intercept on the real part of the X-axis, a semicircle in the middle/high-frequency region, and a straight line in the low-frequency region. The intercept on the X-axis represents the intrinsic resistance (<i>R</i><sub>I</sub>) of the electrolyte and electrode, the diameter of the semicircle represents the charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>CT</sub>) of electrons and Na<sup>+</sup> ions across the electrode/electrolyte interface, and the slope of the straight line indicated as the Warburg resistance (<i>Z</i><sub>w</sub>), which is related to the Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion in the electrode. The HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF electrode has the smallest <i>R</i><sub>I</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>CT</sub> as well as the most steep line before cycling, demonstrating its minimum resistance of Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303102-fig-0004">4a,b</a>). After cycling, the HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF electrode shows the most subtle changes in <i>R</i><sub>I</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>CT</sub>, verifying its best structural stability (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303102-fig-0004">4c,d</a>).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202303102-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7413eea0-beb8-4be6-a36d-141bf31946d7/adfm202303102-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/7413eea0-beb8-4be6-a36d-141bf31946d7/adfm202303102-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/58bbb84a-6012-4135-8275-a7da10d4bdee/adfm202303102-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7413eea0-beb8-4be6-a36d-141bf31946d7/adfm202303102-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202303102-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202303102" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">EIS measurements of PDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO, BDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO, and HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO hybrid electrodes a) before and c) after 500 cycles at 10&nbsp;C. Comparisons of the intrinsic resistance (<i>R</i><sub>I</sub>) and the charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>CT</sub>) of PDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO, BDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO, and HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO hybrid electrodes b) before and d) after 500 cycles at 10&nbsp;<i>C</i>. Inset: The electrical equivalent circuit used for fitting EIS spectra. e) In situ XRD patterns of the HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF hybrid electrode during discharging and charging process in the voltage window of 0.45–3&nbsp;V. f) XPS spectra of V 2<i>p</i> of the HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF hybrid electrode at pristine, discharging to 0.01&nbsp;V and charging to 3&nbsp;V states. g) GITT plots and the calculated <i>D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup></i> values of the HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF hybrid electrode during discharging and charging processes.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <div class="paragraph-element">The Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism of the HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF hybrid electrode is investigated using in situ XRD characterization. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303102-fig-0004">4e</a>, during the whole charging/discharging process, the XRD patterns show negligible changes, demonstrating the outstanding structural stability of the HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO due to the introduction of 1,6-hexanediamine working as a stable pillar to extend the interlayer spacing of V<span class="icomoon"></span>O layers. The evolution of elemental valence state can be investigated via X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) characterization. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303102-fig-0004">4f</a>, the peaks at 517.4, 516.3&nbsp;eV (V 2p<sub>3/2</sub>), and 524.9, 523.6&nbsp;eV (V 2p<sub>1/2</sub>) of pristine HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO belong to V<sup>5+</sup> and V<sup>4+</sup>, respectively. With the embedding of Na<sup>+</sup> (discharging), the valence of V decreases, i.e., the intensity of V<sup>5+</sup> dramatically decreases and that of V<sup>4+</sup> slightly increases, as well as a new peak attributed to V<sup>3+</sup> emerges at 514.1&nbsp;eV. When the electrode is fully charged to 3&nbsp;V, the V<sup>3+</sup> and V<sup>4+</sup> are oxidized, the characteristic peak of V<sup>3+</sup> vanishes and the intensities of V<sup>4+</sup> and V<sup>5+</sup> return to their initial states, revealing a reversible electrochemical reduction process of vanadium. These phenomena are convincingly verifying the outstanding structural stability of the HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF hybrid electrode during the reversible Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction. The number of electron transferred by HDA−VO/NF during the charging and discharging process is calculated to be 2.9. According to the formula, transferring each electron can provide a capacity of 50.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, and thus the HDA−VO/NF can exhibit a redox capacity of 147&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0059_R_d217230257e1224" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - ACS Nano">59</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9a</a> (Supporting Information), the characteristic peaks at 284.8 and 285.8&nbsp;eV in the C 1<i>s</i> spectrum of the pristine HDA−VO/NF electrode belong to the C<span class="icomoon"></span>C and C<span class="icomoon"></span>N bonds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0024_R_d217230257e1235" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Compares to the XPS spectrum of C 1<i>s</i> of the pristine HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO, the emerging broad signal at 288.3/289.4&nbsp;<i>e</i>V of the charged/discharged HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO attributes to the irreversible decomposition of electrolyte, forming carbon-containing ester.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0060_R_d217230257e1244" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Natl Sci Rev">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As for N 1<i>s</i>, the shift and recover of the characteristic peaks reveals the reversible intercalation/extration of Na<sup>+</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9b</a>, Supporting Information). Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9c</a> (Supporting Information) displays the high-resolution O 1<i>s</i> XPS spectra of the pristine and charged/discharged HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO, there are two different oxygen species, i.e., the O<sup>2−</sup> in the V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> lattice and the OH<sup>−</sup>, to correspond to the peaks at the binding energies of 530.30 and 531.6&nbsp;eV. The emerging broad signal at 536.2/535.9&nbsp;eV of the charged/discharged HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO originates from the Auger effect of intercalated and a small amount of residual Na<sup>+</sup>, which indicates the storage/delivery of Na<sup>+</sup> ions. Furthermore, to quantitatively study the transport of Na<sup>+</sup> ions in the HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF electrode during the discharge/charge process, galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) measurement is performed (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303102-fig-0004">4g</a>). The diffusion coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup> ions (<i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>) in the HDA<span class="icomoon"></span>VO/NF electrode can be calculated according to the Fick's second law:<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0061_R_d217230257e1287" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - J. Mater. Chem. A">61</a>, <a href="#adfm202303102-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303102-bib-0062_R_d217230257e1287" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - Electrochim. Acta">62</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202303102-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/acd54296-2e95-4994-8d28-21aef92f4ab0/adfm202303102-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202303102:adfm202303102-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202303102-math-0001.png">
